example, is about 1080; if you were to buy worlds at \$85 trillion a
piece, you would still have trillions of times more worlds than the
number of atoms in the universe; if you were to reduce each world to the
size of an atom, you could buy more than eight hundred and fifty
universes. If, rather than \$1, you were to match the largest
philanthropic donation ever of around \$100 billion by Jamsetji Tata,
your donation would be worth \$1012,849 after a million years, which is
not a lot bigger than \$10 12,837 really.

[Of course, long before you reach this point, **the global economic
system would have collapsed because of your donation**. This
is]{.underline} not meant as a practical suggestion but is supposed [to
reduce to absurdity the idea of a million-year view]{.underline}, and
[it]{.underline} really [**makes a mockery of the** whole **longtermist
motivation** of the effective altruism movement. **The future is simply
too large to think about in any detail**]{.underline}. MacAskill does
not realize just how big a million years is. [**It is unfathomable**.
Everything turns to dust in that kind of time]{.underline}: even a
hundred billion dollars ends up indistinguishable from a single dollar.
[Utilitarianism was criticised for being demanding in requiring
impartiality, but longtermism **takes those demands and puts them on
steroids**. The idea]{.underline} that [we can]{.underline} even [be
morally concerned about what is a million years away, yet again obliged
to do something about it, is **utter folly**]{.underline}. And, of
course, Immanuel Kant tells us that "ought implies can" (1781, 548). The
idea of longtermism is itself absurd, so it is not a surprise after all
that MacAskill is incapable of justifying it.

## Russia Bad

### Russia Bad\-\--2NC

#### Russia is irredeemable\-\--they never came to terms with the violence of Stalin, and failing to understand this dooms the aff. 

Anton **Leist &** Rolf **Zimmermann 24**, Professor of Philosophy at the
University of Z√ºrich, Ph.D. from the University of Frankfurt; Professor

The recent work of Sergei Medvedev is a qualified source for disclosing
dimensions of violence in politics and culture under Putin. Medvedev
speaks not only as a scientific researcher but also as an eyewitness who
published his book The Return of the Russian Leviathan shortly ahead of
the current war (2020 in English; 2017 in Russian). The book describes
and analyses phenomena of violence on various levels. For Medvedev, [the
annexation of Crimea in 2014 means]{.underline} that [Russia's internal
orientation in favour of traditional values]{.underline} --- in Putin's
words, in favour of 'spiritual bonds' --[has]{.underline} now [turned
outwards]{.underline} and [a **new era of international conflict has

> [For too long, we ignored the revanchist rhetoric of]{.underline}
> ‚Äõ[the Russian world]{.underline}'; and now that world has come to us
> in armored personnel carriers. (Medvedev 2020, 14).

Meeting the Russian world under Putin, however, does not only entail
look at a politics of new risk, heroism, and missionary traits. At the

Putin's world. In]{.underline} the course of the war in
[Donbas]{.underline}, Medvedev emphasises under the heading ‚ÄõJihad in

perspective of discussing the Russian way of life as a way of life full

growing]{.underline}. As to the symbols, the parade on Victory Day, 9th

sentence inflicted on]{.underline} members of the band [Pussy Riot in
2012. These]{.underline} actions [are in line with the violation of
accepted norms in the interest of the symbolic assertion of sovereignty
and with the]{.underline} '[state of emergency]{.underline}'. In
Medvedev's view, the annexation of Crimea and subsequent warfare
continues the authoritarian drift of the Russian state seen in the years
leading up to it. This time, though, [the Russian state **declares the
state of emergency in name of the Russian people against i**nternational
**law**]{.underline}. 41

Medvedev also expands the Schmittian theory to shed light on a typical
Russian sociocultural practice. He refers to the virile attitude to
present oneself as a "real lad" (patsan), able to put down or even
humiliate a possible enemy. [The gesture of showing no sign of weakness
is in conjunction with the **willingness to break the
law**]{.underline}, and it marks out a real man from a mere "nerd
('ordinary man')". [Putin's]{.underline} own [past]{.underline} in St.
Petersburg [resembles such a disposition and fits the image of a
**typical Russian disdain for rules and norms**]{.underline}: "In this
way [the logic of the backstreet bandit becomes the logic of Schmitt's
sovereign]{.underline} and the 'code' of the criminal underworld which
formulates contemporary Russian politics." (107)

[This]{.underline} sociocultural diagnosis [corresponds with further
dimensions of violence under the heading of a]{.underline} "**[war for
the body]{.underline}**". A few indications will suffice here: a
[demographic policy]{.underline} is aimed at [pushing up the Russian
birth rate]{.underline} (120) [in accordance with an ideal of a normal
family]{.underline}, specifically a patriarchal family with four
children (136); [the]{.underline} Duma [proposed banning advertisements
for condoms]{.underline} outside specialised publications (128);
[homosexuality is banned]{.underline}, and **[homophobia becomes the
cultural code for anti-Western and anti-globalist beliefs, a
biopolitical creed with fascist analogies]{.underline}** (136). [What is
even more ubiquitous]{.underline}, however, [is the scandalous intensity
of violence against women]{.underline} which became a public issue in
2016 when thousands of women shared their memories of violence they had
suffered, e.g., rape, beatings, and more. As with humiliation and
discrimination in the army, these violent practices take place [under
the ruling patriarchal norm]{.underline}: the strongest lays down the
law and sets the hierarchy (141--143).

Medvedev sees the same code of the rule of the strongest, the unspoken
agreement based on violence and science, operating in politics. Thus,
politics and civil culture go hand in hand: The supposed 'natural right'
of man over woman is paralleled by the right of the state over the
citizens, reaching down to their body. Authorities are free to falsify
the results of elections, the police have no qualms about beating and
torturing suspects, the courts boldly mete out unjust sentences.
Finally, Russia callously moves to annex Crimea (143).

In arguing for a 'de-automatization of violence' by publicly announcing
pain and suppression as in the case of women who claimed their rights,
Medvedev leads us [to a grave problem which remains unsolved in Russia:
**the inability**]{.underline} "**[to speak about trauma]{.underline}**
--- [for example, about the legacy of Stalin's repression]{.underline}"
(144). [This can serve as keyword for the]{.underline} '[war for
memory]{.underline}' and its serious political consequences. [The burden
of de-Stalinization is crucial for the Russian national consciousness
which seems **unable to step out of the long shadows of
Stalinism**]{.underline}. Medvedev combines his diagnosis with an
instructive example worth citing in his own words:

> At the heart of our national consciousness, a black lacuna has formed,
> a sucking void, which the majority of people manage to skate around.
> But then occasionally bones rise to the surface, like on the bank of
> the Kolpashevo River near the Siberian city of Tomsk, where on 1 May
> 1979 the river Ob flooded and burst its banks, opening up a mass grave
> of victims who had been shot in 1937. Thousands well-preserved corpses
> were washed out; and the local authorities ordered that they be
> chopped up by the propellers of the river tugs and washed away.
> (209)42

[The obliviousness of the Russian nation to the traumas of the Stalinist
terror may be explained]{.underline} in part [by the constellation that
Stalinism was not ended by a brutal military defeat]{.underline} like
Nazism in Germany, [but was instead associated with a military
victory]{.underline}, seemingly **[justifying]{.underline}** the **[acts
of repression]{.underline}** committed **[despite their brutality and
the social disasters they brought about]{.underline}**. What is more,
[the regime is guided]{.underline} to this day [by a philosophy and
practice]{.underline}, with few changes or modifications,
[of]{.underline} "[chekism]{.underline}", which is still recognised
today (210). This nearly unbroken tradition of chekism, [alluding to the
Bolshevist secret police]{.underline} which subsequently became the KGB,
of which Putin was an officer, implies that **[there are no guarantees
that terror will not return]{.underline}**. 43 [The tradition of
violence distinctive of Stalinism has survived and shapes the national
consciousness]{.underline}.

This tradition finds its complement in the tradition of suffering as
elaborated in the work of Svetlana Alexievich, who won the Nobel Prize
for Literature in 2015. In her documentary prose, she reports the
stories of victims of political and social violence under Stalinism.
Thus, according to Medvedev, she succeeds in giving the trauma and
memory of Russia's terrifying 20th century a voice. She breaks the
"cultural convention of violence by the state and suffering by the
people, and the sanctification of the victim" (237). No wonder,
therefore, that the work of Alexievich is denounced by mainstream
Russian critics. At the same time, her work calls attention to the
silent acceptance of violence in the context of suffering. 44 This
points to the problem that the identities of the various perpetrators
under Stalinism (e.g., firing squads, investigators, denunciators, etc.)
were kept secret in the USSR after 1956 in exchange for the
rehabilitation of the victims. In light of incriminating evidence, only
about hundred people were found guilty in the time of Khrushchev,
whereas under Brezhnev and Gorbachev the trials were definitively
dismissed (238--239).

This result highlights the problem that [post-Soviet society has failed
to come to terms]{.underline} with [a critical assessment of the
**omnipresent**]{.underline} phenomena of [**violence in the Stalinist
past**. Stalinist violence was not only executed by state agencies and
the Bolshevist party but was also rooted in relevant parts of the
population]{.underline}. In a sense, state violence and violent society
should be seen in a complementary way (Gerlach and Werth 2009). [Going
into details of this constellation]{.underline} would mean initiating
memory processes in a far more elaborate way, to recollect concrete
events and comprehensively reveal the identities of perpetrators and
victims, irrespective of taboos. Evidently, such a course of action
[could only be successful within a political frame defined by standards
of historical responsibility. Instead, Putinism remains **firmly within
Stalinist traditions**]{.underline}, which means that [it resorts to
**suppressing the lawless and violent past**]{.underline}. At the same
time, [the Russian population seems]{.underline} once more
[motivated]{.underline} to a considerable degree [to **honour violent
means**]{.underline} in national and international politics. Thus, [the
political system correlates with socio-cultural dispositions and habits
internalised over generations]{.underline}.

Not surprisingly Medvedev demands to end the "shameful agreement between
the victims and the executioners" and to give priority to legal clarity
regarding Stalinism in the past and the political terror taking place
under Putinism (242). Evidently, [the war against Ukraine]{.underline}
since 24 February 2022 [postpones this postulate for a Russian life in
face of an unpredictable future]{.underline}. That is all the more
reason not to neglect the Russian origin of violence in the continuity
of Putinism and Stalinism.

4.3 The Spectre of Fascism?

The work of Medvedev as well as the previously described issue of
Russian imperial nationalism represent the task of facing Stalinism in
the interest of under- standing the present. This may be underlined by
the recent diagnosis of Nikolai Eppl√©e:

> Thus, coming to terms with the memory of Soviet state terror is not
> pushed aside by the acute need to come to terms with the tragedy of
> today's victims; on the contrary, the two are directly related. [If
> the attack on Ukraine is]{.underline} deeply [motivated by the fact
> that the Russian leadership is **neither willing nor able to give up
> the** Soviet practices of governance and existence, the **imperial and
> colonial legacy of Stalinism**]{.underline}, then [the question of how
> to come to terms with this past is practically **the key problem that
> must be solved in order to suspend the causes of this war once and for
> all**]{.underline}. (Eppl√©e 2023, 13; our translation)

Correspondingly, the problem of violence can be dealt with in a broader
perspective of research on Russia including history, literature, and
politics (Levitt and Novikov 2009). In our context, however, it seems
instructive to pay attention to historical research that inquires into
relations between Stalinism and Nazism, and which modifies and continues
earlier approaches to totalitarianism (e.g., Hannah Arendt). Thus,
besides studies of the political systems, their way of socialisation
(Vergesellschaftung), and the mutual entanglements of these regimes, we
learn of their similar propensity to violence. 45 What is more, we
cannot evade the insight that both systems represent strands of a
modernist development rooted in European history. Therefore, [simplistic
condemnations of]{.underline} these [systems as aberrations in the path
of modernity are **flawed**]{.underline}. Exemplary studies on the
Soviet-Stalinist development in terms of an 'experiment of modernity'
with its violent dimensions remain relevant (Plaggenborg 2006, Chs. 1,
4).

Looking to the present, the latest study of Medvedev, A War made in
Russia (2023), continues his interpretation of Russia as a violent
society in the dimensions already referred to, but also discusses
whether Russia under Putin can be characterised as a version of fascism.
Medvedev refers to Timothy Snyder's well-known thesis (2022), where he
declares Putin's regime 'fascist', but, at the same time, Medvedev
refrains from providing a clear identification with Snyder's thesis. On
the one hand, Medvedev points to [the regime's characteristics of
controlling the body, its cult of the dead and the cult of war as
**typical components of fascism** combined with genocidal tendencies to
**eliminate Ukrainian identity**]{.underline} ('de-Ukrainization'). On
the other hand, however, he rightly observes that [a key element of
fascism is missing: the ability to mobilise society]{.underline}.
Additionally, he remarks that the context for classical fascism was mass
industrialised society, while [Putinism is a result of post-Soviet,
post-industrial, and post-mass society marked by **demobilisation** and
**atomisation**]{.underline}. Whatever comparisons with other
manifestations of fascism may indicate, Medvedev sharpens the view on
the "unmistakably Russian" version which grew out of the decaying
Russian Modernity (Medvedev 2023, 27--31).

This way of framing the question of fascism is in the vein of pre-war
controversies concerning Russia, which were already focusing on the
problem of fascism. Thus, Marlene Laruelle discusses the question Is
Russia fascist? against the background of the widely agreed denominator
of fascism, namely the "utopian project of transformation that operates
by mobilising the masses around a promotion of violence" (Laruelle 2021,
145). Since a fascist mobilisation of society aims at the creation of a
new social order by harking back to a myth of regeneration and a cult of
violence, Laruelle denies that Putin's regime qualify as 'fascist', and
she criticises Snyder's thesis presented in a December 2016 lecture at
Yale University. Instead, she points to the conservative characteristics
of Putinism and the regime's ideological plurality (Ch. 8, 118--141; Ch.
5). This is not to deny fascist tendencies in the Russian Far Right or
Dugin's rehabilitation of fascist doctrine, but the influence of these
threads on Russian policy seems remote. Therefore, Laruelle proposes the
category of "illiberalism" for the Putin regime. This term is designed
not as the opposite of liberalism in the sense of anti-liberalism or
non-liberalism, but more as a kind of post-liberalism, "that is, as an
ideology that pushes back against liberalism after having experienced
it" (Laruelle 2021, 21).

Laruelle agrees the generic approach to fascism developed by Roger
Griffin and concurs with his judgment that Putin is rather a pragmatist
oriented towards Realpolitik, his expansionism notwithstanding. In
contrast, Snyder stands for an argument based on historical analogies.
To consider the pros and cons of both perspectives defines a range of
further research in light of the Russian war against Ukraine, including
revisions of former interpretations. For example, Laruelle's pre-war
analysis may be questioned with regard to her propensity to look at the
Russian state in a rather normalising way. She prefers arguments of
comparison between Putin's regime and Bonapartism (Napoleon III) as well
as the parallel to Berlusconism or her special proposal --- to Gaullism.
Of course, these compari- sons are qualified by differences, but the
course of her argument sounds inspired by reformist hopes that Putin may
be bypassed by reforms as in the case of De Gaulle (Laruelle 2021,
155--156). Thus, the category of illiberalism needs further reflection
in light of current events. 46

Returning to Medvedev and his suggestion of studying fascism in terms of
an "unmistakably Russian" version, we can add the polemical aspect of
speaking of fascism in view of Russia. As Laruelle (2021, 2) rightly
remarks, for the majority of the Russian population, "fascism"
represents the "ultimate evil". Thus, the use of a possible analytical
concept of fascism in light of Russian developments needs careful
reflection. This, of course, does not preclude siding with Medvedev in
diagnosing a somehow paradoxical repetition and reversal of history, in
light of the myth of the 'Great Patriotic War':

> History has come full circle and closed in on itself: those who
> defeated the Nazis have come a pathetic copy of them. Today, the true
> successors of 1941 to 1945 are not the Russian occupiers who have
> invaded a neighbouring country, but the Ukrainians, defending their
> own Motherland. (Medvedev 2023, 104)

In summary, [the challenge for the West consists]{.underline}, in a
sense, [in a historical constellation similar to the resistance to
Nazism during WW II**: to oppose a violent regime that defines itself by
adopting war as a national idea, by using war as a means for
self-affirmation and as an anchor for Russian identity, by conceiving
war as an end in itself**]{.underline} (Medvedev 2023, 152--161). **[The
question of violence in modernity remains a persistent
concern]{.underline}**.

#### Putin is a [textbook dictator]{.underline}, and evidence to the contrary is propaganda. 

Victor Davis **Hanson 22**, Martin and Illie Anderson Senior Fellow at
the Hoover Institution, Ph.D. from Stanford University, "Vladimir Putin,
the Latest of the Failed Irredentists," Independent Institute,
03-07-2022, accessed 07-24-2025,
https://www.independent.org/article/2022/03/07/vladimir-putin-the-latest-of-the-failed-irredentists,
DG

[Irredentism]{.underline}---[the romance of **reclaiming "unredeemed"
old lands**]{.underline}---[is a symptom of **messianic presidents and
premiers**, and **national paranoia and insecurity**]{.underline}.
Leaders demagogue about the recovery of ancient territories that
previously had weakened the nation's imperial grandeur and power.

[Supposedly long-scattered and oppressed peoples with
common]{.underline} linguistic, religious, and cultural [affinities are
recombined]{.underline}---usually [by **violently
overthrowing**]{.underline} their contemporary [**governments** and
**forcing them into a new ethnic super state**]{.underline}. Yet
irredentism is often a one-way street. Supposedly homeless
expatriates---the Greeks of Constantinople, Italians in Malta, Germans
in the Sudetenland, Serbs in Bosnia, and Russians in Ukraine---are said
to be even more zealous nationalists than their kindred in the
motherland. But just as often the territory to be reunited in a grand
imperial scheme can be more reluctant than the would-be uniter.

Early 20th-century Greek romantics fancied resurrecting the old ŒúŒµŒ≥Œ¨ŒªŒ∑
ŒôŒ¥Œ≠Œ± or "Great Idea." That was the dreamy recreation of a panhellenic
Eastern Mediterranean. The New Byzantium was to be ringed by
Greek-speakers in the motherland, Asia Minor, the Aegean Islands,
Cyprus, and northern Egypt.

Yet, like most irredentists, the Greeks never had the manpower or
material wherewithal to reestablish such a modern Byzantine Empire. The
restored 15th century image rested entirely on the opportunistic
implosion of the Ottoman Empire, the 1918 defeat of the Central Powers,
especially in Asia Minor, the Middle East, and the Balkans, the
international chaos following World War I---and the pledges of the
victorious allies.

But soon a new Turkish secular government emerged to undermine the
quixotic Greek effort. The Great Idea's British sponsors betrayed the
project. It ended tragically with thousands of stranded Greeks savagely
butchered throughout Asian Minor.

Pre-Putins

Italian fascist dictator Benito [Mussolini had wilder
dreams]{.underline} in the 1920s and '30s [of recreating the Roman
Empire. In his irredentist fantasies, anywhere Italian was
spoken]{.underline}---[or where Latin once had
been]{.underline}---[there would follow the new Italian
empire]{.underline}. Mussolini discounted the wide-scale poverty in
southern Italy and Sicily and endlessly harangued about the fetters of
British Suez and Gibraltar that unfairly had boxed in the new Rome.

Mussolini's Mare Nostrum---"Our Sea"---would at least remake the
Mediterranean into an Italian lake. A reborn Rome would be flanked by an
Italian-speaking southern Europe, an Italian North and East Africa, an
Italian Aegean, and an Italian Dalmatia and Balkans.

Mussolini could only achieve his dreams through a host of "ifs"---if
France and Britain appeased him, if their Mediterranean navies
disappeared or would not fight, if Nazi Germany threatened Mussolini's
common enemies, if the so-called international community, like the
League of Nations, failed to deter him, and if ultimately Germany won
World War II.

So, Mussolini sequentially grabbed Ethiopia, expanded out from his
Libyan colonies, invaded Egypt, Albania, and Greece---until finally
Britain and America destroyed Mussolini and his fascist fantasies.

Adolf Hitler was the 20th century's most ambitious and most barbaric
irredentist. He came to power by screaming about a drawn-and-quartered
German Reich, carved up by the Versailles Treaty, with millions of
German speakers and lands scattered and lost to his native Austria, to
Poland, to France, and to Czechoslovakia. In Hitler's mind, these were
all "unredeemed" lands that he alone in his genius would reclaim for the
German Volk. He even included the ancient Volga Germans in the distant
domains of the Soviet Union as legitimate claimants on a new Third
Reich.

Unlike Italy, [Hitler had the military, the economy, and the
population]{.underline} for a brief moment [to bully his way into
reclaiming almost every German-speaking minority in Europe and blowing
up the borders of the continent]{.underline}. Finally, Hitler engulfed
the world in a war that cost 70 million dead, by invading all of Europe,
the Soviet Union, North Africa and encouraging Japan to do the same in
Asia and the Pacific. By 1941, the expanded Third Reich numbered over 80
million Germans. It had obliterated Poland and Czechoslovakia. And
Berlin ruled over an area larger in population and territory than the
current European Union. Only Britain was left to be destroyed.

But in truth Germany had already overreached, drunk on easy victories
and blind to the resources and manpower of his new enemies, the Soviet
Union, and the United States. By 1944, the United States alone had
produced a military larger than all the Axis militaries combined, a GDP
larger than those of all the combatants, friend and foe put together,
and a navy larger than all the aggregate navies of the world.

When the wreckage of the war cleared, Hitler's dream was a satanic
irony, as millions of Germans were dead, and millions more expelled from
once annexed nations and forced to walk back into a vastly shrunken
Germany.

With the 1990s breakup of the former Yugoslavia, Serbian strongman
Slobodan Milo≈°eviƒá dreamed of a new Greater Serbia. He sought to force
neighboring Montenegro, Northern Albania, Bosnia, and Herzegovina into a
new version of the 14th century Serbian Empire. Serbia's near
decade-long modern Balkan Wars cost 140,000 dead, earned global
denunciation of Serbia, made Milo≈°eviƒá a hated pariah, and ended with
the independence of all his would-be new conquests.

A Woke West?

[**Putin is history's most recent and first nuclear irredentist**. He
believes **any group of Russian speakers anywhere, or former residents
of imperial Russia or the Soviet Union, or Russian orthodox worshipers,
all belong to Putin's new Russian Empire**. Even if Russian speakers are
independent or happy as minorities]{.underline} in other countries,
[Putin has a **grandiose plan** to force them into his new mother
Russia]{.underline}.

Only that way can a huge new Russia of 270-300 million people, with a
vast area comparable to the old Soviet Union, become again a player on
the superpower stage to rival China and the United States.

[In Putin's mind, he has already forced **Georgia, Crimea**, and
**Eastern Ukraine** back into the Russian fold. Many]{.underline} of the
[old Soviet Republics are]{.underline} already [his **de facto
satellites** or puppets]{.underline}. If he can get back all of Ukraine,
the crown jewel of the old Soviet Union and Mother Russia---41 million
people, 230,000 square miles of territory, the best farmland in Europe,
rich in oil and minerals---Putin feels he would achieve his irredentist
goal. The remaining few lost Russian territories then will either be
easily absorbed, or their puppet governments will obey Russian orders.
Then, he believes, the former Warsaw Pact nations, in terror, will
supposedly shed their NATO alliance or at least become no-fly-zones.

Putin may have initially underestimated the Ukrainian heroic resistance.
He foolishly discounted any chance of NATO defiance. He had no idea how
much the supposedly decadent West still controls the levers and wheels
of the international financial and commercial system now directed at
Russia. He was clueless that new weapons such as cheap drone planes and
improved model Javelins and Stingers put into the hands of relative
amateur shooters could allow them to blow up multimillion-dollar tanks,
and huge trucks full of soldiers---all with a good chance of the shooter
escaping with his life, and the destruction videoed for global social
media consumption.

Now shamed by prior inaction, emboldened by the unexpected Ukrainian
resistance, and egged on by the Churchillian emergence of Volodymyr
Zelenskyy, European Union countries like Germany have suddenly all but
apologized for their former appeasement, nihilist cutbacks of oil and
gas production, and virtual disarmament.

In the United States, the public is ebullient at the scenes of defiant
Ukrainians and hopeful that Putin has at last met his irredentist
Waterloo. The point is not that Putin is stopped for good but that in
theory he could be stopped. And that is something, given the odds
against the Ukrainians and the declinist postmodern West.

In truth, Ukraine is vastly outnumbered, out equipped, and outmanned. It
was armed by the West far too late. It should have received tens of
thousands of anti-tank and anti-aircraft missiles months ago. Last
summer, the United States should have increased daily oil and gas
output, not cut it back. The EastMed pipeline into Italy should have had
the full support of the Biden Administration. Joe Biden should have
stopped with the gratuitous "killer" and "bully" Corn Pop rhetoric,
spoken softly, and instead upped targeted defense spending and stopped
the commissariatization of the Pentagon. Ukraine should have kept out of
U.S. politics and impeachment psychodramas---and especially kept clear
of the toxic Biden family grifting syndicate.

But for now, all those "shoulds" and "coulds" must be considered after
[Putin]{.underline} is stopped. He [believes he can and will eventually
swallow Ukraine, **regardless of the costs in Russian blood and treasure
or the barbaric killing of innocent Ukrainian civilians**. He has
pivoted from the failed "easy" Georgia-Crimea model to the **messy
Chechnya fallback plan**]{.underline}---**[and]{.underline}** thus **[is
going medieval in Ukraine]{.underline}**.

Worse still, [Putin thinks he still can win ugly]{.underline}---maybe
even **[the uglier the better]{.underline}**. Then months from now a
supposedly galvanized West will fear him even more and remember even
less the fate of Ukraine. He assumes Europeans will pivot back and only
recall that by hook or crook the Napoleonic Putin had swallowed
Ukraine---and in the future could do the same anywhere he wished in his
neighborhood.

The leaders of the European Union and NATO are now giving Churchillian
speeches. They promise to retract their prior green nihilism. They
pledge to pump oil and extract more natural gas. They claim they are
through with the appeasing Nord Stream 2 pipeline. They say they will
now welcome a Greek-Cypriot-Israeli EastMed pipeline into southern
Europe. They soar in rhetoric about a new NATO armed to the teeth by
Europeans, shocked by the fate of Ukraine.

Let us pray this is all true.

### Russia Bad\-\--AT: Defensive

The war in Ukraine has exposed the fundamental failure of Western
audiences to appreciate the true nature of modern Russia. Many
international [observers still]{.underline} assume Russia is a rational
actor and [believe]{.underline} policies of [appeasement can
bring]{.underline} the conflict in [Ukraine to an end. **Nothing could
be further from the truth**]{.underline}.

In reality, neither bleeding heart liberals nor diehard realists truly
understand Vladimir Putin. They fail to recognize that he [is an
authoritarian kleptocrat who does not care about Russia's national
interest]{.underline} and is focused instead on his power and wealth.
[He hides]{.underline} this [**self-interest** behind]{.underline} a
fa√ßade of [revisionist Russian nationalism]{.underline} that helps
secure popular support for his criminal rule.

As I have argued in my book, "Russia's Crony Capitalism: The Path from
Market Economy to Kleptocracy," Putin's personal politics combine
authoritarianism and kleptocracy. [He needs war]{.underline} not to make
Russia great again but [to increase his popularity and
justify]{.underline} his [repressive domestic policies]{.underline}.
Putin also fears the rise of a democratic Ukraine and views the
country's Euro-Atlantic integration as an existential threat to his own
authoritarian regime.

Understanding Putin is the key to forming effective Western policies
toward Russia and Ukraine. Ukrainians tend to understand Putin very well
but surprisingly few Westerners do. A number of common misconceptions
need to be addressed and debunked if the West wishes to get Russia
right.

One key problem when dealing with Putin is the Western fear of
escalation. The Russian dictator is well aware of this and always
escalates until he has won or been defeated. [The West must]{.underline}
therefore [**not seek to avoid escalation**, but should demonstrate a
readiness to **escalate more** and **faster**. This is the **only way**

wins**]{.underline}.

Western [fears of]{.underline} "[provoking Putin]{.underline}"
[are]{.underline} particularly **[unhelpful]{.underline}**. For this

Europe and fuel a mood of [excessive caution]{.underline} that **[only
serves to embolden Putin]{.underline}**.

It is difficult to see any logic in these concerns. [Putin]{.underline}
has [started an **unprovoked** and **unjustified war**]{.underline}, but
it is the West that must not provoke him? [Such thinking is]{.underline}
essentially [a call to **allow a Russian victory** and **accept
Ukrainian defeat**]{.underline}. Instead, [the word]{.underline}
"**[provocation]{.underline}**" [should be retired from the]{.underline}
Western [discussion]{.underline} about Putin.

A similarly gentlemanly idea is the notion that the West must allow
[Putin]{.underline} to save face. Really? Putin is no gentleman. He
[wages wars of aggression and ruthlessly orders the destruction
of]{.underline} entire [cities. The West **cannot compromise over crimes
against humanity**]{.underline} on this scale. On the contrary, **[Putin
must be defeated]{.underline}**. [The only language he understands is
the language of **overwhelming strength**]{.underline}.

Many misguided Western politicians and commentators continue to call on
Ukraine to negotiate with Putin. The problem with this position is that
Putin himself has consistently refused to meet, negotiate, or even speak
on the phone with Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelenskyy.

[Putin has]{.underline} also [demonstrated **conclusively**]{.underline}
that **[his word has no value]{.underline}** and [he feels]{.underline}
under [**no obligation** to keep]{.underline} any of his
**[promises]{.underline}**. For decades, [Putin]{.underline} has
[routinely violated international agreement after agreement while
publicly defending his conduct with **bare-faced lies**]{.underline} and
obviously implausible denials. Why bother to conclude another worthless
agreement with this lawless character?

Western calls for Ukrainian neutrality are similarly futile. Ukraine has
been neutral throughout its thirty years of independence and has never
had any serious prospects of joining NATO. Far from fearing Ukraine's
NATO integration, Putin invaded the country precisely because it was not
a NATO member and had not received sufficient military support from the
alliance.

NATO membership remains the only credible security guarantee for
Ukraine. Finland and Sweden have drawn the obvious conclusion from
Russia's invasion and have now abandoned decades of neutrality. Ruling
out closer ties between NATO and Ukraine will only encourage further
Russian aggression in Ukraine and elsewhere.

When [advocates of appeasement]{.underline} run out of other arguments,
they tend to [call for an immediate ceasefire]{.underline}. While
seemingly sensible, [these appeals ignore Russia's **long record** of
treating ceasefire agreements as **opportunities to regroup** and
**prepare for new attacks**]{.underline}.

This trend was particularly prominent during the seven years of
simmering conflict in eastern Ukraine following the signing of the
second Minsk Accords [in]{.underline} February [2015]{.underline}.
[Russia **refused to adhere to the ceasefire terms**]{.underline} agreed
[in Minsk and regularly launched localized escalations]{.underline} to
suit Moscow's interests.

Peaceniks often declare that the West must avoid painting Putin into a
corner. Similarly, they warn against destabilizing Russia, but [it
is]{.underline} entirely [irrational to position Putin as a source of
stability when he is **clearly** the main destabilizing factor
in]{.underline} both [Europe and Russia itself]{.underline}. Where would
Europe be today if this twisted logic had been applied to Adolf Hitler?

Russia's war against Ukraine is the biggest geopolitical crisis for a
generation. It is entirely understandable that many international
observers are desperate to end the slaughter and believe a negotiated
peace is the best way to secure peace. However, such hopes are
shortsighted and fail to appreciate the character of the Putin regime.

[The only way to end the war]{.underline} conclusively [is via a

since 2014. [If a compromise peace allows Putin to hold]{.underline} on

the borders of [Ukraine]{.underline}.

[Years of Western wishful thinking and concessions to the Kremlin have
**failed to prevent Putin's emergence** as the **greatest threat to

and **belatedly abandon the path of appeasement**]{.underline}.

#### If Russia is defensive, neg on presumption\-\--you can't make Putin trust us.

**Dylan et al. 24**, Huw Dylan, Reader in Intelligence and International
Security at King's College London, Ph.D. from the University of
Aberystwyth; David V. Gioe, Associate Professor of History at the United
States Military Academy, Ph.D. in Politics and International Studies

https://doi.org/10.1177/13691481241258108, DG

[Russia's paranoia]{.underline} about spies and subversives predates
Putin. It both [derives from and helped foster a strategic
culture]{.underline} -- t[he Russian approach to viewing and managing
threats]{.underline}, whether real or imagined -- [in which its security
apparatus has traditionally focused inwards, **views the external world
as almost implacably hostile**, and that]{.underline}, as a consequence,
to quote Yaacov Falkov (2022), '[granted the]{.underline} \[[Russian
Intelligence Services]{.underline}\] [the most exalted position among
other Soviet strategic tools]{.underline}'. It was to
(counter)intelligence that Soviet leaders turned almost reflexively to

the guilty and innocent alike in its hunt for the]{.underline} '[enemies

In 1986, just a few years before the collapse of the Soviet experiment,
[the KGB persisted in]{.underline} its traditional role of [targeting

The strategic culture, the norms, mindsets, and institutional memories

**[norms]{.underline}**. By the turn of the millennium,

Identifying the precise tipping point in Russia's descent into Putinist
autarchy is a matter of judgement. However, following the 1999 apartment

political or personal, was squeezed inexorably. This applied to

complicity in the 1999 bombings and other outrages, and of being mired
in rampant corruption more generally, were assassinated, some rather
theatrically like former FSB officer Alexander Litvinenko who was
poisoned with Polonium-210 in London in 2006, which was 'probably
approved by Putin' according to British authorities (Gioe et al., 2019).
Politicians who questioned and investigated the bombings were murdered,
as were Sergei Yushenkov and Yuri Shchekochikhin in 2003. Serious
political challengers were also subjected to unrelenting intimidation
and violence. Boris Nemtsov was gunned down in the shadow of the Kremlin
in February 2015. Putin's most potent political threat, the campaigner
Alexey Navalny survived an attempted assassination in 2020 but died in
February 2024 while languishing in Putin's gulag, his funeral becoming a
contested locus of protest.

[For Putin, the definition of a traitor encompassed far more than
turncoat]{.underline} as [**he conflates** independence or **criticality
with subversion and disloyalty**]{.underline}. The natural corollary of
this is that [**all dissent is a threat**, a potential nucleus for an
alternative node of political power]{.underline} -- [and]{.underline}

Matters for the attention of the intelligence services have]{.underline}

### Russia Bad\-\--AT: Defensive\-\--Liberal Order

#### Russia doesn't care about recognition by the liberal order nor are they interested in signals of U.S. good faith. 

Michael **Kimmage &** Jeremy **Shapiro 24**, Professor of History at the
Catholic University of America, Ph.D. in American Studies from Harvard
University; Research Director at the European Council on Foreign
Relations, M.A. from the Johns Hopkins University School of Advanced
International Studies, "The Myths That Warp How America Sees
Russia---and Vice Versa," Foreign Affairs, 01-25-2024,
https://www.foreignaffairs.com/united-states/myths-warp-how-america-sees-russia-and-vice-versa

In Russia, it is conventional wisdom that the United States is
power-mad. [The American public]{.underline}, many [Russians believe, is
under the thumb of a **megalomaniacal** U.S. elite. Enthusiasm for a
liberal international order gets **little traction** in
Russia]{.underline} not because all Russians are realists but [because
their mythic view of the United States reduces the liberal]{.underline}
international [order to a **vehicle of American ambition**]{.underline}.
Many Russians are convinced that U.S. leaders' references to a
supranational web of norms, laws, and partnerships are merely
smokescreens for the cooptation that lies at the core of American
foreign policy.

[The reigning Russian myth is of Soviet vintage. According to this
myth]{.underline}, during the Cold War, [American capitalist elites
wanted to **run the world**]{.underline} and found innumerable military
pretexts to exert their wishes. The nightmare purportedly began after
World War II, when the United States rewired the political codes in
Japan and Germany, pushed those countries into alliances dominated by
the United States, used them as staging grounds for U.S. military
operations, and compelled them to serve as cheerleaders for the U.S.
national interest. To keep up, the Soviet Union had to build a bulwark
of "friendly countries" in Eastern Europe and establish its own global
footprint, lest the perfidious United States advance uncontested.

The United States' global influence during this era was real. But the
[Soviet characterization]{.underline} was a caricature---and one that
[proved **enduring**]{.underline}. Even after the Cold War ended,
[according to the Russian myth, the **U**nited **S**tates kept
**seducing**]{.underline} others with false rhetoric, including
**[Russia's neighbors]{.underline}**---countries such as Poland,
Romania, and the Baltic states. In this telling, U.S. allies operate
more as instruments of American power than as independent states. Where
governments resisted---in Afghanistan, Iraq, Libya, Serbia, and Syria,
as well as in Ukraine before the 2014 Maidan uprising---[regime change
has been the American preference]{.underline}. Hegemony by invitation,
[**heg**emony **at the barrel of gun**]{.underline}: the means may vary,
but the end is never in question.

[Moscow]{.underline} has paid a high price for holding onto this myth.
It has [obligated itself to **contend with the American monster** even
at the expense of becoming more dependent on China]{.underline}. The
EU's Eastern Partnership program, which led to Ukraine's Maidan
uprising, was an authentic expression of idealism about the country's
European future, not a covert exercise of American hegemony. But the
contention that the CIA had staged a coup in Ukraine was a lie that
Russians had long been primed to believe. [Even if Russia's **top
leaders knew this** claim **was false**]{.underline}, their [public
insistence on it **closed off moderate responses**]{.underline} (such as
accommodating the new government in Kyiv) [and made more extreme
options]{.underline} (such as annexing Crimea) seem
**[necessary]{.underline}**.

In general, [the myth of a **U**nited **S**tates drunk on power and
unwilling to stick to agreements makes it very **hard for Moscow to
negotiate** over regional questions]{.underline}. [Russians cannot
imagine]{.underline} that the [leaders of countries such as Ukraine
**have minds of their own**]{.underline}. For Moscow, Ukrainian
hostility is simply the veiled extension of American hostility, and
American hostility toward Russia demands equal Russian hostility toward
the United States. If the only language the United States understands is
power, then negotiation, deliberation, and the granting of concessions
all entail undue risk.

### Russia Bad\-\--AT: Defensive\-\--Mearsheimer

#### They should lose for spreading disinformation about Russia.

Filip **Kostelka 22**, Professor and Chair in Political and Social
Change at the Department of Political and Social Sciences of the
European University Institute, Ph.D. in Political Science from Sciences
Po, "John Mearsheimer's lecture on Ukraine: Why he is wrong and what are
the consequences," EUIdeas, 07-11-2022,
https://euideas.eui.eu/2022/07/11/john-mearsheimers-lecture-on-ukraine-why-he-is-wrong-and-what-are-the-consequences

On 16 June 2022, John Mearsheimer, Professor at the University of
Chicago, delivered a lecture on the war in Ukraine at the Robert Schuman
Centre of the European University Institute. During [the]{.underline}
lecture, Mearsheimer defended his controversial [view that Russia's
invasion of Ukraine is the fault of the **U**nited **S**tates and
NATO]{.underline}. For scholars studying Central and Eastern Europe and
the region's citizens, the lecture [is **deeply
problematic**]{.underline} on factual, scientific, and moral grounds.
This article addresses the validity of Mearsheimer's central thesis, the
quality of the presented evidence, and the lecture's broader
implications and the concept of academics' social responsibility. It
argues that Mearsheimer's explanation of the war in Ukraine is
intellectually unsatisfactory and that it rests on shaky empirical
foundations. [By publicly defending his]{.underline} scientifically
[unsound thesis, **Mearsheimer legitimises Russia's propaganda** and
**violates the fundamental values of social
responsibility**]{.underline} that **[all academics should
respect]{.underline}**.

A controversial thesis

Mearsheimer's main point is that the United Statesand its allies are to
blame for Russia's invasion of Ukraine since they allegedly pushed for
Ukraine's NATO membership, the prospect of which Russia has seen as an
existential threat. There are at least four reasons why this account is
wanting.

First, it ignores the fact that [Ukrainians]{.underline} -- like other
Eastern Europeans -- [have been actively seeking NATO membership to
protect themselves]{.underline} from the Russian threat. [They did not
need to be pushed]{.underline}, they have desperately wanted to join.
They first officially applied for membership in 2008 and repeatedly
declared it a policy priority after 2014. However, in Mearsheimer's
account, Ukrainians appear only as victims of Russia's invasion,
deprived of any agency. [Ascribing to them a uniquely passive role is
an]{.underline} analytical shortcoming that turns the blame game on its
head, and an [illustration of how **condescendingly**]{.underline} some
[Western academics and pundits regard Central and Eastern Europeans: as
**clueless pawns** in a]{.underline} geopolitical [game played
by]{.underline} the "[great]{.underline}" [powers]{.underline}.

Second, Mearsheimer's account is at least partially incomplete since, in
isolation, [it cannot]{.underline} satisfactorily [explain the
**timing** of the invasion or why other pro-Western countries in
Russia's immediate neighbourhood have avoided a similar
fate]{.underline}. When Russia's invasion started, it still appeared
extremely unlikely that Ukraine would join NATO in the foreseeable
future. What is more, the prospect that NATO, in the implausible
scenario of Ukrainian membership, would launch an attack against a
nuclear power is foolish. Indeed, Ukraine joining NATO would hardly be a
credible military threat to Russia and, if Crimea remained in Russian
hands, Russia's key strategic interests would be largely preserved. All
this suggests that [any serious explanation]{.underline} of the invasion
[needs to consider]{.underline} additional factors such as [Russia's
domestic political situation; the ideological and symbolic threat a
democratic and prosperous Ukraine would represent]{.underline} to
Russia's incumbent political regime; [and the potential desire of an
ageing dictator to conquer]{.underline} immortality [through territorial
expansion]{.underline}. Without considering these factors and assessing
them against solid empirical evidence, we will never understand what
triggered the invasion.

Third, Mearsheimer's explanation draws on his own version of the realist
theory of international relations, offensive realism, which is not an
overly reliable guide to the behaviour of contemporary states. Offensive
realism holds that great powers such as Russia cannot tolerate perceived
security threats in their neighbourhoods. However, here as elsewhere,
offensive realism often fails on empirical grounds. The breakup of the
Soviet Bloc, the post-Cold War military weakness of Germany, and peace
among major European powers are just a few examples of such failures.
Even if Russia really considered the prospect of Ukraine's accession to
NATO as an existential threat, which is far from certain despite
Russia's official rhetoric, there was absolutely no certainty that it
would react in the way it did to Ukraine's sovereign decision to seek
joining the alliance. It is not by accident that the invasion took many
members of Russia's political establishment by surprise. Given the
variety of alternative scenarios that could unfold, blaming the United
States, NATO, or even Ukraine -- if we acknowledge its active pursuit of
NATO membership -- for the war is not only morally wrong (i.e., wars are
always started by those who pull the trigger, not those who join a
defensive military alliance), but it is also intellectually
unsatisfactory.

Fourth, one would hope that such a controversial thesis would be borne
out by strong empirical evidence. Yet, the [evidence]{.underline}
presented during the lecture largely [boils down to an **uncritical
reading** of **selective official statements made by the Russian
leadership**]{.underline}. Furthermore, [the justification of the use of
this]{.underline} "[evidence]{.underline}", [referring to the **alleged
sincerity of Russia's president**, reveals further **cracks in the
cred**ibility **and scientific value of the** central
**argument**]{.underline}.

Weakness of the empirical evidence

Why should one believe what Russia's leaders say? "Because Putin rarely
lies to foreign audiences", claimed Mearsheimer. He went on to pre-empt
disagreements by reminding the lecture hall that he had authored a book
on lying in international politics, which finds that political leaders
lie to other countries much less often than we think. This attempt to
mute criticism regrettably failed to mention that the book is not based
on systematic research (page 12 of the book); that lying is rare
particularly for democracies like the United States (p. 13), which
Russia is not; and that the frequency of lying largely depends on the
definition of what lying is (p. 26), which is somewhat restrictive in
the book at hand. During his talk, Mearsheimer thus remained oblivious
to Russia's numerous lies on public record, including Putin's original
denial of any involvement in Crimea in 2014, which was followed by open
boasting about the annexation a few months later. The US Department of
State even went so far as to officially publish two 10-item lists of
documented Russia's falsehoods on Ukraine in 2014.

Mearsheimer's lack of critical scrutiny of Russia's statements contrasts
with his refusal to consider that Russia could have imperial ambitions
and that the invasion's objective could (also) be territorial. While he
was willing to take selected statements on the existential threat by
Putin at face value, he would accept the imperial motivation only if
somebody proved that Putin "thought it was a desirable goal, \[...\] a
feasible goal, \[...\] \[and\] that he intended to pursue that goal". It
is hard to imagine what kind of evidence Mearsheimer would like to see
as Putin has been quite clear in his repeated pre-invasion statements,
denying the legitimacy and even the very existence of the independent
Ukrainian state. On the eve of the invasion, Putin explicitly argued
that Ukraine never had "real statehood," and said it was an integral
part of Russia's "own history, culture, spiritual space." After the
invasion, he went on to compare himself to the 18th century Tsar, Peter
the Great, and to declare that Russia was simply reclaiming its
territory.

Engaging in curious intellectual gymnastics, Mearsheimer defended his
central thesis by claiming that while Putin's objectives escalated
during the invasion into imperial ambitions, Russia originally did not
want to annex territory before the invasion. Why? "Because there were
only 190,000 soldiers in Russia's invading army, which is far too small
a force to vanquish and occupy Ukraine". Yet again, this argument does
not hold much water when we remember that Russia clearly targeted Kiev
from the first day of the invasion and that it suffered terrible
military losses arguably due to poor intelligence. All available
evidence points to a disastrous miscalculation by the Kremlin leadership
consisting, among others, in a serious over-estimation of the
effectiveness of its own army, and of the popular support for Russia
within Ukraine. Russian may have assembled fewer troops than would be
normally necessary for controlling a country the size of Ukraine because
it expected little resistance. Its military operations were supposed to
be backed by a network of Ukrainian collaborators, most of whom,
however, may have existed only in reports prepared by Russia's security
officials. According to Ukrainian official sources, which certainly need
to be interpreted carefully in wartime, Putin discovered that his secret
services may have embezzled \$5 billion allocated from the Russian
budget for subversive operations in Ukraine between 2014 and 2022.

More generally, [denying the plausibility of Russia's imperial
objectives]{.underline} oddly [clashes with]{.underline} the core tenets
of Mearsheimer's own theory and a large amount of circumstantial
[**ev**idence]{.underline} from Central and Eastern Europe. Offensive
realism argues that great powers aim to maximise their material
capabilities. If Russian intelligence reports suggested Ukrainians would
not resists their invaders, why wouldn't Putin want to annex Ukraine's
territory? And why would his plans escalate from intervention to
annexation when the invasion did not go as planned, as Mearsheimer
claims? On the contrary, such escalation would have made much more sense
if the invasion had proceeded smoothly. By [questioning Russia's
imperial ambitions]{.underline}, Mearsheimer [turns a blind eye to the
nostalgia for the Soviet empire in Russian public opinion, the
persistence of a hierarchical and imperial worldview among Russian
elites]{.underline} and in the Russian media, [and Russia's meddling
in]{.underline} the politics of [Central European
countries]{.underline}. Moreover, in the months leading up to the
invasion, Russia required a Ukrainian pledge to not join NATO, but also
a NATO pledge to withdraw all troops from the territories of its
post-1990 members in Central and Eastern Europe. Clearly, Russia's
ambitions do not stop with Ukraine. That is fully in line with offensive
realism, but it runs counter to Mearsheimer's current thesis, which
implies that if the US did not push for Ukraine's NATO membership, there
would be no "crisis."

Consequences and academics' social responsibility

Mearsheimer's determined promotion of his controversial view is hard to
understand. Whether it is motivated by his attachment to the realist
theory, a taste for provocation, or even some proximity to Russia's
interests is hard to tell. Nevertheless, his account has limited
explanatory power and little scientific value. [It inter alia leads to
theoretical inconsistencies, relies on **cherry-picking** of **official
statements** made by a serial liar]{.underline} in international
relations, [**sets double standards** when assessing available evidence,
and uses **rhetorical gymnastics** to address unfavourable new
realities]{.underline}. What is enrobed in a scientific cloak is
punditry with far too serious real-life consequences. [It **plays into
the hands of Russian propaganda**, which the Kremlin does not hesitate
to instrumentalise]{.underline}. Mearsheimer clearly does not realise or
care how socially detrimental his questionable claims are.

While the right to express unpopular academic ideas needs to be
defended, their [authors are **responsible for the
consequences**]{.underline}. They should always weigh the strength of
the supporting evidence, the ideas' potential benefits to society, and
the likely repercussions of expressing them outside purely academic
circles. [When the evidence is weak, societal benefits are low, and the
repercussions are disastrous]{.underline} (such as legitimising a
criminal invasion), [academics should **choose a different
topic**]{.underline} for touring the world's lecture halls.

#### Total fool. 

Alexander J. **Motyl 23**, Professor of Political Science at Rutgers
University-Newark, Ph.D. in Political Science from Columbia University,
"America's leading 'realist' keeps getting Russia wrong," The Hill,
07-04-2023,
https://thehill.com/opinion/international/4079666-americas-leading-realist-keeps-getting-russia-wrong

John J. Mearsheimer, the University of Chicago's realist foreign policy
guru, published his latest analysis of the Russian-Ukrainian War on a
historic day: June 23, when Russia was roiled by a failed putsch led by
the Wagner Group's Yevgeny Prigozhin.

Just as Russian President Vladimir Putin's fate, as well as that of his
regime, hung in the balance, just as a successful coup could have
altered the course of the war, led to civil bloodshed and the possible
disintegration of the Russian Federation --- all scenarios invoked by
Putin in his address to the nation --- [Mearsheimer failed to give any
attention]{.underline} whatsoever [to Russia's **domestic politics**,
preferring to focus]{.underline} instead, as he always does, exclusively
[on the Russia state's relations with Ukraine and the West]{.underline}.

[This would be like ignoring domestic factors in the U.S. conduct of the
wars in Vietnam, Iraq and Afghanistan; French and British hopes for
appeasement]{.underline} in 1938; [the role of power struggles and
ideology in the Soviet decisions to invade, Hungary, Czechoslovakia, and
Afghanistan]{.underline} --- [and]{.underline}, of course, [Putin's
**very personal decision** to launch a poorly planned all-out attack on
Ukraine in 2022]{.underline}.

Prigozhin's march on Moscow, though unsuccessful, revealed to Russians
and the world that the Russian state and regime are brittle, that Putin
is increasingly vulnerable, and that the proximate and ultimate outcome
of the war depends as much on what Russians do within their state as on
what they do to Ukrainians with their state.

[Mearsheimer's oversight is **no accident.** Domestic factors are
irrelevant to his theoretical model]{.underline}. That doesn't mean that
he has no insights to make that are worthy of attention; it does mean
that [his predictions and policy recommendations are **so utterly
divorced from anything resembling reality as to be wrong a
priori**]{.underline}.

Putin, for one, would agree. His fateful decision to invade Ukraine was
based exclusively on Mearsheimerian balance of power calculations.
Putin, like Mearsheimer, saw that Russia had a larger military, economy
and population than Ukraine. Naturally, it would win, and quickly --- a
view that many policymakers and analysts inspired by Mearsheimer's
realism also shared. Had America's Founding Fathers been realists of
Mearsheimer's ilk, they would never have dared to declare independence
from the reigning global superpower in 1776.

Unsurprisingly, although Mearsheimer's lengthy paper has 68 endnotes,
not a single one references a Russian- or Ukrainian-language source.
Given such limitations, [Mearsheimer cannot access the views of real
Russians or Ukrainians on the left, right, and center]{.underline} ---
[who might have alerted him to the brittleness of the regime and the
weakness of Putin]{.underline} --- [and must]{.underline} therefore
[rely on and take at face value the foreign-policy statements of Russian
leaders and their propagandists]{.underline}.

[There is]{.underline}, after all, [**no other way** to buttress his
claims about Russia's supposed fear of NATO and the West. Russian- and
Ukrainian-language sources]{.underline}, as well as Sovietologists and
post-Sovietologists, [might have reminded Mearsheimer that Russian
leaders habitually engage in **mendacity**, a tradition that goes back
at least as far as]{.underline} Vladimir [Lenin, who promised to
liberate non-Russians and instead imposed a colonial yoke on
them]{.underline}.

[Putin and his comrades]{.underline} see Nazis where there aren't any:
Ukraine. They [see militarization **where there isn't
any**]{.underline}: ditto. They insisted in late 2021 and early 2022
that they would never, ever invade Ukraine --- and then did. [They
deny]{.underline}, still, [targeting Ukrainian civilian
targets]{.underline}. They deny, still, having destroyed the Kakhovka
dam. The list could go on ad infinitum.

[And yet]{.underline}, bizarrely, [when Russians say they
fear]{.underline} Ukrainian membership in [NATO and U.S. nuclear
weapons]{.underline} on Ukraine's eastern border, [Mearsheimer believes
them, when he should be asking]{.underline} himself [whether
such]{.underline} ostensible [fears have **any grounding in reality**
and what the real reasons might be]{.underline}.

I've made these points many times. For starters, **[absolutely no
one]{.underline}**, including the Russians and Ukrainians, [expected
Ukraine to become a NATO member for]{.underline} at least **[20
years]{.underline}**. And [in 2014]{.underline}, the year the war
actually began, [there was no talk of Ukraine's joining]{.underline}, by
either the Ukrainians, the West or the Russians. Ukraine didn't matter
to the West's perceptions of its security. In contrast, [Ukraine
mattered to Russian perceptions]{.underline}, not because of any actual
military threat --- Ukraine had some 6,000 battle-ready troops in 2014
--- [but because of the ideological threat the democratic Maidan
Revolution posed to Putin's claims to be a legitimate
autocrat]{.underline}.

Obviously, official Russian statements would never admit to such an
interpretation. Russian-language analysts, and their Ukrainian
counterparts, could easily have briefed Mearsheimer and helped correct
his myopia.

Mearsheimer notes in his analysis that, "I am attempting to predict the
future, which is not easy to do, given that we live in an uncertain
world. Thus, I am not arguing that I have the truth; in fact, some of my
claims may be proved wrong."

Indeed, he doesn't, and indeed, they will.

### Russia Bad\-\--AT: Regime Collapse Bad

#### Both the risk of collapse [itself]{.underline} and the outcome are [fabricated]{.underline} by Russia.

Polina **Sinovets &** D. **Shultz 23**, Associate Professor of
International Relations and Head of the Odesa Center for
Nonproliferation at Odesa I. I. Mechnykov National University, Ph.D. in
Political Science from the Institute of World Economy and International
Relations; MINDS Fellow at the NATO Defense College, "Russia's Nuclear
Propaganda: From the Cold War to Ukraine," The Journal of Slavic
Military Studies, Vol. 36, No. 4, 2023, accessed 07-24-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/13518046.2023.2297586, DG

This line of effort seeks to portray any viable replacement for Putin as
less rational and more aggressive. It is based on the Kremlin's twin
assumptions that the West's objective is to seed color revolution and
regime change in Russia and that Russia's strategic defeat in Ukraine
may threaten Putin's domestic hold on power.

Though the concept of 'strategic defeat' is not clearly defined, [Putin
portrays regime change and Russia's collapse as the West's final
goal]{.underline} in the current war.67 Consequently, [much of the
Kremlin's cognitive warfare output targeting the Western public and
policymakers aims to **grow fear** and **uncertainty** over]{.underline}
the nature of [a post-Putin Russia and portray Putin as the least bad
option]{.underline} for the West. [These]{.underline} efforts [amplify
pre-existing fears of regime collapse]{.underline} or civil turmoil in
Russia among some Western policymakers, and what this could mean for the
security of Russia's nuclear arsenal, coupled with the notion that
Russian defeat in Ukraine is likely to result in Putin being replaced.68
[The Kremlin frequently bolsters this]{.underline} narrative [by having
**figures other than Putin**]{.underline} (such as popular TV hosts or
politicians) **[seed the most extreme nuclear threats, which Putin
himself subsequently walks back]{.underline}**, giving him an image of
relative calm and rationality. For example, when questioned following
Sergey Karaganov's inflammatory article calling for Russia to engage in
preemptive nuclear strikes, Putin responded: 'I reject this \[idea\]. .
. . First, we see no need to use it \[nuclear strikes\]; and second,
considering this, even as a possibility, factors into lowering the

overtly referenced by Dmitry Medvedev during the civil turmoil
associated with the Wagner mutiny in June 2023, stating 'We will not let
events develop according to a scenario in which nuclear weapons end up
in the hands of \[Wagner\] bandits --- however much the \[Wagner\]
criminals who have lost their minds want this'.70 In fact, Yevgeny

\[The Ukrainians\] were neighbours . . . a nuclear bomb is like \[an\]
axe. You shouldn't run after your neighbour with an axe. Better to do it
honestly: either beat him or admit that he beat you. The place to prove
you're right is on the battlefield'.71

[These lines of effort are **designed to convince Western policymakers
to minimize support** for Ukraine, lest Ukrainian]{.underline}
'catastrophic [success]{.underline}' result in a [collapse]{.underline}
of [Putin's regime]{.underline} and his replacement by a more escalatory
figure. **[In reality]{.underline}**, from our perspective, [Putin's
loss of power in Russia is more likely to result in a **collapse of
Russia's war effort** in Ukraine **than a nuclear escalation**. Putin's
encouragement of competing power factions]{.underline} seeking his
approval [means]{.underline} that [whichever]{.underline} individual or
[faction gains power after Putin will likely be consumed with the need
to consolidate]{.underline} domestic [power by overcoming adversary
factions]{.underline}.72 Accomplishing [this]{.underline} objective [is
contra-indicated with **squandering military resources in Ukraine, or by
starting an external nuclear war**. Historical precedents bear this out:
civil turmoil]{.underline} in the 1905 and 1917 Russian revolutions, and
the collapse of the Soviet Union in 1991, all [amplified by humiliating

and Afghanistan respectively), [resulted in an **inward turn rather than
an escalation of external conflicts**, as domestic stakeholders sought
to consolidate]{.underline} internal [control. This inward
turn]{.underline} (at least, over the short and medium term) [following
the collapses of the Russian Empire]{.underline} (1917) [and the Soviet
Union]{.underline} (1991) then [provided opportunities for]{.underline}
many [colonized states]{.underline} of the Russian Empire and USSR [to
achieve independence]{.underline}.

[As for]{.underline} similar Western [fears around the security of
Russia's nuclear arsenal]{.underline} in a context of civil turmoil
following regime collapse, [historical precedent]{.underline} again
[shows a different outcome. **The Soviet Union did collapse in
1991**]{.underline}, while **[in possession of the largest nuclear
arsenal in the world]{.underline}**. Despite breaking up into multiple
independent states, [there was no]{.underline} large-scale [civil war
and no]{.underline} major [nuclear-related disasters]{.underline}. While

to ensure this transition and prevent proliferation, this
[effort]{.underline} was successful and [led to a **relative d√©tente**
